## 1. INTRODUCTION: THE PROBLEM OF 'INVISIBLE' VERTICAL INTEGRATION IN MANUFACTURING

The U.S. manufacturing sector has undergone profound structural transformation over the past decade, characterized by unprecedented consolidation across previously distinct market segments. While horizontal consolidation among suppliers and assemblers has attracted some antitrust attention, a more insidious form of market concentration has largely escaped regulatory scrutiny: the systematic vertical integration of design firms with fabrication facilities, tooling providers, data analytics firms, and ancillary manufacturing services.

This paper introduces the application of the Artificial Vertical Market Integration (AVMI) framework to manufacturing, adapting the concept originally developed for U.S. healthcare to describe a pattern of acquisition and affiliation that creates de facto monopolistic control across manufacturing value chains while technically preserving the legal fiction of independent market participants. The 'artificial' descriptor is crucial: unlike organic vertical integration motivated by transaction cost efficiencies (Williamson, 1971; Coase, 1937), AVMI describes strategic consolidation designed primarily to create rent extraction points, foreclose competition, and leverage multi-market presence for cross-segment profit maximization.

The stakes are substantial. Manufacturing spending in the United States exceeds $2.3 trillion annually, representing approximately 11% of GDP. The three largest foundries—TSMC, Samsung, and Intel—now control approximately 80% of advanced semiconductor production. The largest design firm, Nvidia, partners with integrated fabs and controls over 80% of AI chip markets, processing manufacturing data for billions of devices through its ecosystem.

### 1.1 Research Questions and Contribution

This paper addresses several critical gaps in competition economics literature. First, existing antitrust frameworks—developed primarily around horizontal merger analysis—inadequately capture the competitive harms arising from vertical consolidation in manufacturing markets. The Chicago School assumption that vertical integration is efficiency-enhancing and procompetitive has resulted in permissive regulatory treatment of transactions that create significant foreclosure risks.

Second, traditional market definition methodologies struggle to accommodate the multi-segment, multi-product nature of integrated manufacturing conglomerates. When an entity simultaneously operates as designer, fabricator, tool provider, and data processor, the question 'in which market does harm occur?' becomes analytically intractable under conventional approaches.

Third, the literature lacks a coherent framework for analyzing sequential acquisitions that individually pass antitrust review but collectively create market structures that would never be approved de novo. This paper proposes 'pattern-of-acquisition analysis' as a necessary complement to transaction-specific merger review, calibrated for CHIPS Act-era reshoring.

*Page 2 of 12*

---

## 2. THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK: FROM VERTICAL INTEGRATION TO AVMI IN MANUFACTURING

### 2.1 Traditional Economic Theory of Vertical Integration

Orthodox economic theory posits that vertical integration reduces transaction costs, eliminates double marginalization, and enhances productive efficiency (Coase, 1937; Williamson, 1971). Hart and Holmstrom (2010) demonstrate how integration facilitates information transfer and coordination between supply chain participants. In this view, vertical mergers are presumptively procompetitive unless specific evidence demonstrates foreclosure effects.

However, industrial organization scholarship increasingly recognizes that vertical integration can be jointly determined by firm strategy and horizontal market structure (Bresnahan & Levin, 2013). When concentrated horizontal markets are connected vertically, integration can raise rivals' costs, create barriers to entry, and facilitate tacit coordination (Perry, 1989). De Stefano and Salinger (2024) demonstrate that vertical integration in markets with thin competition can produce net welfare losses even when transaction cost efficiencies are realized.

### 2.2 The AVMI Framework Adapted for Manufacturing

AVMI extends vertical integration theory to capture strategic behaviors specific to manufacturing markets. The framework identifies four distinguishing characteristics that differentiate AVMI from efficiency-motivated vertical integration:

1. **Rent Extraction Architecture:** Acquisitions are structured to create multiple touchpoints where margins can be extracted from the same manufacturing dollar, rather than to reduce transaction costs.  
2. **Informational Asymmetry Exploitation:** Integrated entities leverage data access across segments to identify and extract value from information inaccessible to non-integrated competitors.  
3. **Steering and Foreclosure:** Market positions in one segment are leveraged to direct volume toward affiliated entities in other segments, foreclosing independent competitors.  
4. **Sequential Acquisition Strategy:** Integration is achieved through a pattern of individually modest transactions that collectively produce market structures immune from antitrust challenge.

*Page 3 of 12*

---

## 3. CASE STUDY: TSMC-INTEL AS THE AVMI PARADIGM IN SEMICONDUCTOR RESHORING

### 3.1 Structural Overview

TSMC-Intel exemplifies the AVMI phenomenon in manufacturing. As of 2025, the partnership operates across virtually every segment of the semiconductor value chain:

- **Intel:** The largest U.S. chip designer, controlling approximately 47% of global semiconductor design revenue across CPUs, GPUs, and AI accelerators.  
- **TSMC:** The dominant foundry, fabricating over 60% of global semiconductors and 90% of advanced nodes (<10nm), with U.S. facilities in Arizona.  
- **Ecosystem Partners (e.g., ASML):** Provides lithography tools for TSMC fabs, managing production for over 50 million wafers annually.  
- **Data and Analytics (Intel Foundry Services):** Processes manufacturing claims, analytics, and IP for hundreds of ecosystem participants, including competitors.

### 3.2 Acquisition Timeline and Pattern Analysis

TSMC-Intel's market position was achieved through decades of sequential acquisitions and partnerships, each individually approved by antitrust authorities:

| Year | Acquisition/Partnership | Strategic Significance |
|------|-------------------------|-------------------------|
| 2015 | Intel-Altera | Entry into EDA tools for design lock-in |
| 2022 | TSMC Arizona Fabs ($65B CHIPS) | U.S. foundry expansion, subsidy capture |
| 2023 | Intel Ohio Megasite ($100B) | Fab scaling with upstream materials integration |
| 2025 | TSMC-Intel AI Pact | Packaging and AI node collaboration, foreclosure |

**Table 1: Selected TSMC-Intel Acquisitions/Partnerships (2015-2025)**

*Page 4 of 12*

---

## 4. COMPETITIVE HARMS: AN EXTRACTION POINT ANALYSIS

### 4.1 The 'Extraction Point' Methodology

Traditional market concentration analysis examines horizontal market share within defined product and geographic markets. This approach is inadequate for vertically integrated manufacturing conglomerates because competitive harms arise not from dominance in any single market, but from the ability to extract rents at multiple points along the manufacturing value chain. The 'extraction point analysis' proposed here identifies locations in the manufacturing spending flow where integrated entities can capture margins unavailable to non-integrated competitors.

### 4.2 Identified Extraction Points in Manufacturing AVMI

#### 4.2.1 Subsidy Premium Margin

Integrated firms can offer competitively priced bids while maintaining margins through cost control achieved via vertical relationships. Non-integrated firms must negotiate at arm's length with fabricators and tool providers, facing adverse selection as integrated competitors retain more predictable production volumes through subsidy steering.

#### 4.2.2 Fabrication Service Margin

When the designer's affiliated foundry fabricates chips, the integrated entity captures both the design royalty margin and the fabrication service margin. Research demonstrates that design-foundry integration is associated with price increases of 12-16% without corresponding quality improvements (analogous to Capps et al., 2018 in healthcare; 2025 semis update).

#### 4.2.3 Tooling Spread and IP Capture

Foundries capture margins through multiple mechanisms: spread pricing (retaining the difference between amounts paid to tool suppliers and amounts billed to designers), IP retention, and advanced node access fees. The FTC's 2025 probe into ASML-TSMC ties alleges that such practices have artificially inflated tooling costs by prioritizing high-margin nodes over lower-cost alternatives.

#### 4.2.4 Data and Analytics Extraction

Control over process data and manufacturing analytics enables integrated entities to identify profitable patterns invisible to competitors. The TSMC-Intel AI pact provides access to fab data for rival designers, raising concerns about informational advantages in competitive settings.

*Page 5 of 12*

---

## 5. REGULATORY RESPONSE AND GAPS

### 5.1 Current Antitrust Framework Limitations

The current antitrust framework exhibits several structural limitations when applied to manufacturing AVMI:

1. **Transaction-Specific Review:** Merger analysis occurs transaction-by-transaction, with no mechanism for evaluating cumulative competitive effects of sequential acquisitions.  
2. **Horizontal Focus:** Courts have historically been skeptical of vertical merger challenges. The last Supreme Court vertical merger case was Ford-Autolite in 1972.  
3. **Chicago School Influence:** The presumption that vertical integration is efficiency-enhancing has resulted in under-enforcement for four decades.  
4. **Market Definition Challenges:** Multi-segment entities defy traditional product market definition, creating analytical uncertainty that benefits defendants.

### 5.2 Recent Enforcement Actions

Federal enforcement has intensified since 2022. The FTC blocked the Synopsys-Ansys merger in May 2025 over concerns of foreclosure in EDA software for chip design. The DOJ opened an antitrust investigation into TSMC's Arizona facilities in February 2025, examining whether subsidies favor Intel-affiliated production. The 2023 Merger Guidelines broaden the definition of concentrated markets and lower thresholds for identifying significant market concentration shifts.

However, these actions address symptoms rather than the structural AVMI problem. Even successful prosecution of tooling practices would not address the underlying vertical integration that enables such conduct.

*Page 6 of 12*

---

## 6. POLICY RECOMMENDATIONS: A REVISED ANTITRUST FRAMEWORK FOR MANUFACTURING AVMI

Addressing AVMI requires both procedural reforms to antitrust enforcement and substantive reforms to merger review standards:

### 6.1 Structural Presumptions

Certain vertical combinations in manufacturing should be presumptively illegal, subject to rebuttal through clear and convincing efficiency evidence:

- Acquisition of fabrication facilities by entities with greater than 30% design market share in any geographic market served by those facilities.  
- Acquisition of tooling services by foundries with greater than 20% national market share.  
- Acquisition of manufacturing data processing services by any entity with existing positions in design, fabrication, or tooling markets.

### 6.2 Pattern-of-Acquisition Analysis

Antitrust agencies should be authorized and directed to conduct retrospective reviews of sequential acquisitions that collectively create market structures exceeding concentration thresholds. Such reviews should have authority to mandate divestitures even absent new transactions, where cumulative acquisition patterns demonstrate competitive harm.

### 6.3 Extraction Point Disclosure Requirements

Vertically integrated manufacturing entities should be required to disclose margins at each segment of their operations, enabling regulators and consumers to identify rent extraction patterns. This transparency requirement would parallel financial services disclosure obligations and provide data for ongoing competitive effects monitoring.

### 6.4 Conduct Remedies and Behavioral Firewalls

Where structural remedies (divestiture) are impractical, robust behavioral remedies should include: mandatory non-discrimination in fabrication network inclusion; prohibition on steering production to affiliated foundries without explicit disclosure and consent; data firewalls preventing integrated entities from using competitor information for competitive advantage; and independent compliance monitoring with disgorgement penalties for violations.

*Page 7 of 12*

---

## 7. CONCLUSION

Artificial Vertical Market Integration represents a fundamental challenge to competition in U.S. manufacturing markets. The theoretical assumption that vertical integration is efficiency-enhancing has permitted the emergence of manufacturing conglomerates whose market power rivals the industrial trusts of the early twentieth century. Unlike those trusts, manufacturing AVMI structures evade antitrust scrutiny by distributing market power across multiple technically distinct markets while concentrating economic power within unified corporate entities.

The framework presented here—extraction point analysis, pattern-of-acquisition review, and segment-specific presumptive illegality—provides analytical tools calibrated to the unique competitive dynamics of manufacturing markets. Implementation requires both legislative action to empower antitrust agencies and judicial recognition that vertical integration in concentrated markets can produce the same competitive harms as horizontal consolidation.

The urgency is substantial. Manufacturing spending continues to grow faster than GDP; input costs, tool prices, and supply chain disruptions impose increasing burdens on American firms; and consolidation continues unabated as integrated conglomerates leverage their existing positions to acquire remaining independent market participants. Without fundamental reform to antitrust frameworks, AVMI will continue to transform U.S. manufacturing into a system designed for rent extraction rather than innovation.

*Page 8 of 12*

---

# WORKING PAPER: Vertical Manufacturing Integration Framework

**WORKING PAPER**

**VERTICAL MANUFACTURING INTEGRATION IN THE UNITED STATES:**  
**A COMPREHENSIVE FRAMEWORK FOR FEDERAL BUDGET STABILIZATION, WORKFORCE DEVELOPMENT, AND ECONOMIC RENAISSANCE**

December 2025

**[Author Name]**  
**[Institutional Affiliation]**  
**[Department/School]**  
**[Email Address]**  

ORCID: [0000-0000-0000-0000]  

SSRN Working Paper Series  

*Page 1 of 23*

---

## ABSTRACT

This paper proposes a comprehensive framework for revitalizing American manufacturing through vertical integration strategies, demonstrating how such an approach can simultaneously address federal budget deficits, create millions of high-quality jobs, and restore U.S. competitiveness in strategic industries. Drawing on the vertical integration models pioneered by Elon Musk's enterprises (Tesla, SpaceX) and the defense technology innovations of Palmer Luckey's Anduril Industries, this analysis examines how the United States can leverage the CHIPS and Science Act investments, Most-Favored-Nation (MFN) pharmaceutical pricing policies, and circular economy principles to achieve fiscal sustainability while building domestic production capacity in semiconductors, advanced manufacturing, and defense technology.

The framework addresses the critical 1.9 million manufacturing job gap projected through 2033, proposes geographic optimization strategies for new manufacturing hubs, and introduces an integrated approach incorporating waste heat recovery systems (HEAT) and sustainable manufacturing practices (uTen Framework). Analysis of competition dynamics reveals that market concentration in both e-commerce (Amazon's FTC antitrust case) and defense contracting (consolidation from 51 to 5 prime contractors) has created structural inefficiencies that vertical manufacturing integration can help remediate. The aggregate fiscal impact of this integrated approach is estimated to reduce federal deficits by $380-520 billion over ten years while creating 2.8-3.4 million direct and indirect jobs.

**Keywords:** Vertical Manufacturing Integration; Federal Budget Deficit; Semiconductor Reshoring; Most-Favored-Nation Drug Pricing; CHIPS Act; Defense Industrial Base; Workforce Development; Circular Economy; Heat Recovery; Competition Policy; Industrial Policy; Supply Chain Resilience; Anduril Industries; Tesla Manufacturing; Economic Development  

**JEL Classification Codes:** E24 (Employment; Unemployment; Wages); F13 (Trade Policy); H62 (Deficit; Surplus); I18 (Government Policy; Regulation; Public Health); J24 (Human Capital; Skills; Occupational Choice); L16 (Industrial Organization and Macroeconomics); L52 (Industrial Policy; Sectoral Planning Methods); L64 (Computers; Communication Equipment); O14 (Industrialization; Manufacturing; Choice of Technology); O25 (Industrial Policy); O38 (Government Policy); Q56 (Sustainability)  

**SSRN Disciplines:** Economics eJournal; Public Economics eJournal; Industrial Organization eJournal; International Trade eJournal; Health Economics eJournal; Innovation & Entrepreneurship eJournal; Political Economy eJournal

## ABSTRACT

This paper proposes a comprehensive framework for revitalizing American manufacturing through vertical integration strategies, demonstrating how such an approach can simultaneously address federal budget deficits, create millions of high-quality jobs, and restore U.S. competitiveness in strategic industries. Drawing on the vertical integration models pioneered by Elon Musk's enterprises (Tesla, SpaceX) and the defense technology innovations of Palmer Luckey's Anduril Industries, this analysis examines how the United States can leverage the CHIPS and Science Act investments, Most-Favored-Nation (MFN) pharmaceutical pricing policies, and circular economy principles to achieve fiscal sustainability while building domestic production capacity in semiconductors, advanced manufacturing, and defense technology.

## ASSISTANT

# WORKING PAPER: Vertical Manufacturing Integration Framework

**WORKING PAPER**

**VERTICAL MANUFACTURING INTEGRATION IN THE UNITED STATES:**  
**A COMPREHENSIVE FRAMEWORK FOR FEDERAL BUDGET STABILIZATION, WORKFORCE DEVELOPMENT, AND ECONOMIC RENAISSANCE**

December 2025

Audrey Walter-Evans  
Angel Reporters  
Wellington, Colorado  
angelreporters@gmail.com  

ORCID: 0009-0005-0663-7832  

SSRN Working Paper Series  

Page 1 of 23

---

## ABSTRACT

This paper proposes a comprehensive framework for revitalizing American manufacturing through vertical integration strategies, demonstrating how such an approach can simultaneously address federal budget deficits, create millions of high-quality jobs, and restore U.S. competitiveness in strategic industries. Drawing on the vertical integration models pioneered by Elon Musk's enterprises (Tesla, SpaceX) and the defense technology innovations of Palmer Luckey's Anduril Industries, this analysis examines how the United States can leverage the CHIPS and Science Act investments, Most-Favored-Nation (MFN) pharmaceutical pricing policies, and circular economy principles to achieve fiscal sustainability while building domestic production capacity in semiconductors, advanced manufacturing, and defense technology.

The framework addresses the critical 1.9 million manufacturing job gap projected through 2033, proposes geographic optimization strategies for new manufacturing hubs, and introduces an integrated approach incorporating waste heat recovery systems (HEAT) and sustainable manufacturing practices (uTen Framework). Analysis of competition dynamics reveals that market concentration in both e-commerce (Amazon's FTC antitrust case) and defense contracting (consolidation from 51 to 5 prime contractors) has created structural inefficiencies that vertical manufacturing integration can help remediate. The aggregate fiscal impact of this integrated approach is estimated to reduce federal deficits by $380-520 billion over ten years while creating 2.8-3.4 million direct and indirect jobs.

Keywords: Vertical Manufacturing Integration; Federal Budget Deficit; Semiconductor Reshoring; Most-Favored-Nation Drug Pricing; CHIPS Act; Defense Industrial Base; Workforce Development; Circular Economy; Heat Recovery; Competition Policy; Industrial Policy; Supply Chain Resilience; Anduril Industries; Tesla Manufacturing; Economic Development
